[1mุงููุฑุงุฌุน ูุงููุฑุงุกุงุช ุงูุฅุถุงููุฉ [0m

In addition to the giants on whose shoulders I have stood for more than 30 years and who are recognized in the text and 
bibliography that follows, I should like to thank the many archivists and librarians in the United States, Australia, and the 
United Kingdom who are all too often taken for granted. I should also like to thank my former students Jason Flanagan and Jacqui 
Bird for their thoughtful analyses of the career of J. Robert Oppenheimer and the role of atomic scientists in the making of 
nuclear weapons, respectively. Needless to say, there is a vast literature on various aspects of the politics of nuclear weaponry 
and the problems and prospects of dealing with nuclear weapons. The publications listed below represent the tip of that iceberg 
and include the most important studies in English. Constraints of space have made it necessary to omit many excellent and 
important works in the field. 

[1mุงููุตู ุงูุฃูู [0m

The best place to begin the study of nuclear weapons is the pages of the [3mBulletin of Atomic Scientists [0m, founded in 1945 as a 
newsletter distributed among nuclear physicists concerned by the possibility of nuclear war; for 60 years the [3mBulletinโs [0mDoomsday 
Clock has followed the rise and fall of nuclear tensions. For a discussion of the far-reaching effects of nuclear weapons, 
including the mass fire caused by firestorms, see Lynn Eden, [3mWhole World on Fire: Organizations, Knowledge, and Nuclear Weapons 
Devastation [0m(Cornell University Press, 2004). The best introduction to the threat posed by nuclear terrorists is Graham Allison, [3m
Nuclear Terrorism: The Ultimate Preventable Catastrophe [0m(Times Books, 2004); also useful are Scott D. Sagan and Kenneth N. Waltz, [3m
The Spread of Nuclear Weapons: A Debate Renewed [0m(W. W. Norton, 2003) and Joseph Cirincione, Jon Wolfstahl, and Miriam Rajkumar, [3m
Deadly Arsenals: Nuclear, Biological and Chemical Threats [0m(Carnegie Endowment for International Peace, 2005). 

[1mุงููุตู ุงูุซุงูู [0m

The Einstein letter together with FDRโs reply is found in Vincent C. Jones, [3mManhattan: The Army and the Atomic Bomb [0m(US Government 
Printing Office, 1985). A good introduction to the Einstein story, taking into account all the latest discoveries of letters and 
organized in a conventional chronological format, is Walter Isaacson, [3mEinstein: His Life and Universe [0m(Simon and Schuster, 2007). 
For details on the German research effort, see Richard Rhodes, [3mThe Making of the Atomic Bomb [0m(Simon and Schuster, 1986); McGeorge 
Bundy, [3mDanger and Survival: Choices about the Bomb in the First Fifty Years [0m(Random House, 1988); Mark Walker, [3mGerman National 
Socialism and the Quest for Nuclear Power, 1939โ1949 [0m(Cambridge University Press, 1989); and Jeffey T. Richelson, [3mSpying on the 
Bomb: American Nuclear Intelligence from Nazi Germany to Iran and North Korea [0m(Norton, 2006). The best introduction to the making 
of the atomic bomb is still Henry D. Smyth, [3mA General Account of the Development of Methods of Using Atomic Energy for Military 
Purposes under the Auspices of the United States Government, 1940โ1945 [0m(US Government Printing Office, 1945). 

For the role of the atomic scientists, see Robert Jungk, [3mBrighter Than a Thousand Suns: The Moral and Political History of the 
Atomic Scientists [0m(Victor Gollancz, 1958); Robert Gilpin, [3mAmerican Scientists and Nuclear Weapons Policy [0m(Princeton University 
Press, 1962); and Gregg Herken, [3mBrotherhood of the Bomb: The Tangled Lives and Loyalties of Robert Oppenheimer, Ernest Lawrence, 
Edward Teller [0m(Henry Holt, 2002). Also see Jacqueline Birdโs related essay in [3mThe Politics of Nuclear Weaponry [0m, ed. Richard Dean 
Burns and Joseph M. Siracusa (Regina Books, 2007). 

The โatomic diplomacyโ debate may be followed in Gar Alperovitz, [3mThe Decision to Use the Atomic Bomb and the Architecture of an 
American Debate [0m(Harper Collins, 1995); Robert James Maddox, [3mWeapons for Victory: The Hiroshima Decision Fifty Years Later [0m
(University of Missouri Press, 1995); and, most recently, Wilson D. Miscamble, [3mFrom Roosevelt to Truman: Potsdam, Hiroshima, and 
the Cold War [0m(Cambridge University Press, 2007). For a useful distillation of the vast literature on this subject, see J. Samuel 
Walker, [3mPrompt and Utter Destruction: Truman and the Use of Atomic Bombs against Japan [0m(University of North Carolina Press, 1997). 

For the impact of wartime bombing of civilians, see Jorg Friedich, [3mFire: The Bombing of Germany, 1940โ45 [0m(Columbia University 
Press, 2007), and the incomparable John Hersey, [3mHiroshima [0m(Penguin, 1946). The George Orwell quote is found in [3mOrwell in Tribune: 
โAs I Pleaseโ and Other Writings, 1943โ7 [0m, compiled and edited by Paul Anderson (Politiocoโs, 2007). 

[1mุงููุตู ุงูุซุงูุซ [0m

The Baruch Plan and the Gromyko proposal are found in Joseph M. Siracusa, [3mThe American Diplomatic Revolution: A Documentary 
History of the Cold War, 1941โ1947 [0m(Holt, Rinehart and Winston, 1976). For a detailed treatment of the Baruch Plan, which contains 
a fair amount of primary source material, see Leneice N. Wuโs essay in Richard Dean Burns (ed.), [3mEncyclopaedia of Arms Control and 
Disarmament [0m(3 vols, Charles Scribnerโs Sons, 1993), and Richard G. Hewlett and Oscar E. Anderson, Jr, [3mA History of the United 
States Atomic Energy Commission, vol. 1, The New World, 1939/1946 [0m(University of Pennsylvania, 1962). 

The best historical assessments include Barton J. Bernstein, โThe Quest for Security: American Foreign Policy and International 
Control of Atomic Energyโ, [3mJournal of American History 60 [0m(March 1974), 1003โ44; and Larry Gerber, โThe Baruch Plan and the 
Origins of the Cold Warโ, [3mDiplomatic History 6 [0m(Winter 1982), 69โ95. The Bush quote is found in the introduction of Thomas C. 
Reedโs At the Abyss: [3mAn Insiderโs History of the Cold War [0m(Ballantine Books, 2004). 

[1mุงููุตู ุงูุฑุงุจุน [0m

Portions of this chapter have been adapted from my recent study, with David G. Coleman, [3mReal-World Nuclear Deterrence: The Making 
of International Strategy [0m(Praeger Security International, 2006). The famous NSC 68 document is found in Joseph M. Siracusa, [3mInto 
the Dark House: American Diplomacy and the Ideological Origins of the Cold War [0m(Regina Books, 1998). 

For the historical context of these years, see Paul Boyer, [3mBy the Bombโs Early Light: American Thought and Culture at the Dawn of 
the Atomic Age [0m(North Carolina Press, 1994); Michael Mandelbaum, [3mThe Nuclear Revolution: International Politics before and after 
Hiroshima [0m(Cambridge University Press, 1981); and Michael J. Hogan, [3mA Cross of Iron: Harry S. Truman and the Origins of the 
National Security States, 1945โ1954 [0m(Cambridge University Press, 1998). 

Also useful are Gregg Herken, [3mThe Winning Weapon: The Atomic Bomb in the Cold War, 1945โ1950 [0m(Knopf, 1980); Margaret Gowing, [3m
Independence and Deterrence: Britain and Atomic Energy, 1945โ1952 [0m(Macmillan, 1974); and John Lewis Gaddis et al. (eds) [3mCold War 
Statesmen Confront the Bomb [0m(Oxford University Press, 1999). 

The Soviet side of this story is told ably in David Holloway, [3mStalin and the Bomb: The Soviet Union and Atomic Energy, 1939โ1956 [0m
(Yale University Press, 1994); and Vojtech Mastny, [3mThe Cold War and Soviet Insecurity: The Stalin Years [0m(Oxford University Press, 
1996). 

The growth of the global anti-nuclear campaign, together with the forces, personalities, and events that moulded it, is told in 
Lawrence S. Wittnerโs incomparable multi-volume study, [3mThe Struggle against the Bomb [0m(Stanford University Press, 1993). 

[1mุงููุตู ุงูุฎุงูุณ [0m

Indispensable are three works by Raymond L. Garthoff: [3mDeterrence and the Revolution in Soviet Military Doctrine [0m(Brookings 
Institution, 1990), [3mSoviet Strategy in the Nuclear Age [0m(rev. edn, Praeger, 1962), and [3mDรฉtente and Confrontation: American Soviet 
Relations from Nixon to Reagan [0m(Brookings Institution, 1985). In this same category I also include Lawrence Freedmanโs [3mThe 
Evolution of Nuclear Strategy [0m, 3rd edn (Palgrave Macmillan, 2003) and [3mDeterrence [0m(Polity, 2004). 

Still useful on nuclear doctrine are Thomas C. Schelling, [3mArms and Influence [0m(Yale University Press, 1966), and Glenn H. Snyder, [3m
Deterrence and Defence [0m(Princeton University Press, 1961). 

Of the several accounts of the Cuban Missile Crisis, Graham Allison and Philip Zelikowโs [3mEssence of Decision: Explaining the Cuban 
Missile Crisis [0m, 2nd edn (Longman, 1999) is the standard account. Alexandr Fursenko and Timothy Naftali, [3mโOne Hell of a Gambleโ: 
Khrushchev, Castro and Kennedy, 1958โ1964, Secret History of the Cuban Missile Crisis [0m(W. W. Norton, 1997), had unprecedented 
access to the former Soviet archives, while Ernest R. May and Philip Zelikow, [3mThe Kennedy Tapes: Inside the White House during the 
Cuban Missile Crisis [0m(Belknap Press, 1997), provides transcripts of most of the audio recordings JFK secretly made during the 
episode. 

The treaty milestones of these years are covered in Joseph M. Siracusa and David G. Coleman, [3mDepression to Cold War: A History of 
America from Herbert Hoover to Ronald Reagan [0m(Praeger, 2002), and Richard Dean Burns (ed.), [3mEncyclopaedia of Arms Control and 
Disarmament [0m(3 vols, Charles Scribnerโs sons, 1993). 

Arms control efforts to limit the potential threat of strategic weaponry may be found in McGeorge Bundy, [3mDanger and Survival; [0mJ. 
P. G. Freeman, [3mBritainโs Nuclear Arms Control Policy in the Context of Anglo-American Relations, 1957โ68 [0m(St Martinโs Press, 
1986); Martin Goldstein, [3mArms Control and Military Preparedness from Truman to Bush [0m(Peter Lang, 1993); and Robin Ranger, [3mArms and 
Politics, 1958โ1978: Arms Control in a Changing Political Context [0m(Gage, 1979). Kennedyโs creation of the Arms Control and
